<div id="review_179401092" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/179401092" />
    <a title="Elizabeth (Alaska)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2026178-elizabeth-alaska"><img alt="Elizabeth (Alaska)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1304966095p2/2026178.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/179401092?book_show_action=true">Jun 24, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Elizabeth (Alaska)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Elizabeth (Alaska)" href="/user/show/2026178-elizabeth-alaska">Elizabeth (Alaska)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2026178-elizabeth-alaska?shelf=1001-books">1001-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2026178-elizabeth-alaska?shelf=mid-20th-century">mid-20th-century</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer179401092" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10281012709692260343">First posting 6/11/2014 for <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/16116.A_Question_of_Upbringing__A_Dance_to_the_Music_of_Time___1_' title='A Question of Upbringing (A Dance to the Music of Time, #1) by Anthony Powell' rel='nofollow noopener'>A Question of Upbringing</a><br/>Second posting 7/29/2014 for <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1006997.A_Buyer_s_Market__A_Dance_to_the_Music_of_Time__2_' title='A Buyer's Market (A Dance to the Music of Time #2) by Anthony Powell' rel='nofollow noopener'>A Buyer's Market</a><br/>Final Posting 8/19/2014 for <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2467144.The_Acceptance_World__A_Dance_to_the_Music_of_Time___3_' title='The Acceptance World (A Dance to the Music of Time, #3) by Anthony Powell' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Acceptance World</a><br/><br/>This edition includes the first three books of the 12-volume series. I'll "review" them individually, as that is how I'm reading them.<br/><br/><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/16116.A_Question_of_Upbringing__A_Dance_to_the_Music_of_Time___1_' title='A Question of Upbringing (A Dance to the Music of Time, #1) by Anthony Powell' rel='nofollow noopener'>A Question of Upbringing</a> introduces us to who I assume will be the four main characters throughout the series. It is told in the first person by Jenkins (first name as yet unknown) and begins in "about the year 1921." The</span>
  <span id="freeText10281012709692260343" style="display:none">First posting 6/11/2014 for <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/16116.A_Question_of_Upbringing__A_Dance_to_the_Music_of_Time___1_" title="A Question of Upbringing (A Dance to the Music of Time, #1) by Anthony Powell" rel="nofollow noopener">A Question of Upbringing</a><br />Second posting 7/29/2014 for <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1006997.A_Buyer_s_Market__A_Dance_to_the_Music_of_Time__2_" title="A Buyer's Market (A Dance to the Music of Time #2) by Anthony Powell" rel="nofollow noopener">A Buyer's Market</a><br />Final Posting 8/19/2014 for <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2467144.The_Acceptance_World__A_Dance_to_the_Music_of_Time___3_" title="The Acceptance World (A Dance to the Music of Time, #3) by Anthony Powell" rel="nofollow noopener">The Acceptance World</a><br /><br />This edition includes the first three books of the 12-volume series. I'll "review" them individually, as that is how I'm reading them.<br /><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/16116.A_Question_of_Upbringing__A_Dance_to_the_Music_of_Time___1_" title="A Question of Upbringing (A Dance to the Music of Time, #1) by Anthony Powell" rel="nofollow noopener">A Question of Upbringing</a> introduces us to who I assume will be the four main characters throughout the series. It is told in the first person by Jenkins (first name as yet unknown) and begins in "about the year 1921." The "boys" are apparently in the last years of public school. Widmerpool is at least a year older than Stringham, Templer, and Jenkins. Each has sufficient family resources to be in public school, but the differences are apparent.<br /><br />There is not much plot, just a telling of life as it happens. I don't expect the series to be very deep, nor especially emotional, but a very pleasant read being the times and lives of just the sort that appeals to me.<br /><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1006997.A_Buyer_s_Market__A_Dance_to_the_Music_of_Time__2_" title="A Buyer's Market (A Dance to the Music of Time #2) by Anthony Powell" rel="nofollow noopener">A Buyer's Market</a> tells us the narrator is Nicholas "Nick" Jenkins. That might have been in the first story and I missed it. The boys are now definitely men and out in the world. I think these will just be a pleasant interlude to heavier reading. I think they are on the 1001 list because of the insight into this wealthier class of people in the years between the wars and a way of life that probably no longer exists.<br /><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2467144.The_Acceptance_World__A_Dance_to_the_Music_of_Time___3_" title="The Acceptance World (A Dance to the Music of Time, #3) by Anthony Powell" rel="nofollow noopener">The Acceptance World</a> is the final installment in this first 3-volume book of the series. I feel as if I'm settling into Nick Jenkins telling his story. He makes observations about people - and he knows a lot of them. Their personalities are varied. They are mostly people who are somewhat affluent, or come from affluent families. His characters try to keep up the appearance of affluence, even when they no longer have any real hope of being such. This story takes place in the Depression, or the slump. Jenkins is a writer - yes, this is semi-autobiographical - and his contacts for the most part move in the world of letters and art.<br /><br />I'm giving it 4 stars at this point, but I think it resides at the lower part of that group. I look forward to the rest of the series, although I may have to lay it aside for a few months. </span>
  <a data-text-id="10281012709692260343" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2385968491" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2385968491" />
    <a title="Robert Sheard" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1863589-robert-sheard"><img alt="Robert Sheard" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1581875935p2/1863589.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2385968491?book_show_action=true">Jun 07, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Robert Sheard" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Robert Sheard" href="/user/show/1863589-robert-sheard">Robert Sheard</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2385968491" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13333509182145733400"><i>A Question of Upbringing</i> is the first novel of a twelve-novel sequence called <i>A Dance to the Music of Time</i>. It has been described as the English equivalent of Marcel Proust’s <i>Remembrance of Things Past</i> and its books have been called quintessentially English novels.<br/><br/>Nicholas Jenkins attends an upper-class boarding school (based on Powell’s own experience at Eton), where he befriends two older students, Charles Stringham and Peter Templer. <br/>Separated into four chapters, each section follows Jenkins </span>
  <span id="freeText13333509182145733400" style="display:none"><i>A Question of Upbringing</i> is the first novel of a twelve-novel sequence called <i>A Dance to the Music of Time</i>. It has been described as the English equivalent of Marcel Proust’s <i>Remembrance of Things Past</i> and its books have been called quintessentially English novels.<br /><br />Nicholas Jenkins attends an upper-class boarding school (based on Powell’s own experience at Eton), where he befriends two older students, Charles Stringham and Peter Templer. <br />Separated into four chapters, each section follows Jenkins at a different phase early in life. The first chapter outlines his fall semester and his growing association with Stringham and Templer, as well as some mention of another acquaintance, Kenneth Widmerpool.<br /><br />The second chapter is the winter holidays. Stringham is finished with school and is going to spend the spring in Kenya with his father, but before he leaves he takes Jenkins with him to his mother’s and step-father’s home for the break.<br /><br />The third chapter is after Jenkins finishes school and spends time in France, studying the language and learning more about life before he goes up to university. In France he crosses paths once again with Widmerpool.<br /><br />And the final chapter is at university. Templer doesn’t attend, but comes for a visit with some friends from London. And Stringham decides to leave university early to work in London. But by the end of the chapter, Jenkins realizes he probably won’t see either of them much in the future.<br /><br />The entire novel feels very much like a prologue to the volumes that come later, but as a stand-alone work, there’s not much to recommend it. Nothing really happens, and Jenkins spends the entire 230 pages making observations about the many people he meets (and who will presumably appear again in later volumes), and commenting upon the changeable nature of friendships and romances. Decent enough theme, but no real story to go with it at this stage.<br /><br />I wasn’t a particular fan of Powell’s style, so when coupled with the absence of a plot, this wasn’t a successful experience for me. I’m pretty firmly in the camp, from what I’ve read, that the series is vastly overrated. I won’t be going on to volume two.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13333509182145733400" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer208205943" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer1286517775902568028">A Dance to the Music of Time is a delicious book: I am loving every minute of reading it. Originally comprising 12 separate novels published from 1951 to 1975 it now comes in four volumes and I’ve only read the first volume so far, but I am hooked. <br/><br/>Sometimes compared to Proust’s A La Recherche du Temps Perdu (Remembrance of Lost Time), Anthony Powell’s masterpiece might also be called a comedy of manners. It is much easier to read than Proust, and not just because the sentences are shorter: it’s</span>
  <span id="freeText1286517775902568028" style="display:none">A Dance to the Music of Time is a delicious book: I am loving every minute of reading it. Originally comprising 12 separate novels published from 1951 to 1975 it now comes in four volumes and I’ve only read the first volume so far, but I am hooked. <br /><br />Sometimes compared to Proust’s A La Recherche du Temps Perdu (Remembrance of Lost Time), Anthony Powell’s masterpiece might also be called a comedy of manners. It is much easier to read than Proust, and not just because the sentences are shorter: it’s more amusing, less angst-ridden, and the ‘Britishness’ of its subtle ironies is part of its charm.<br /> <br />A clumsy summary might make this work seem like a soap-opera, so I shan’t try except to say that the novels follow the fortunes of a group of young men through their adolescence and adulthood, from the immediate post WW1 period to the early 1970s. It begins when Jenkins, Templer, Stringham and Widmerpool are in the same house at Eton, and it moves on as they muddle their desultory way through and out of university and then into careers of one sort or another. They muddle into and out of relationships too: love, business, artistic and so on. Along the way there is a veritable cavalcade of eccentric and sometimes dubious characters from all walks of life.<br /><br />To see the rest of my review please visit <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://anzlitlovers.com/2012/01/22/a-dance-to-the-music-of-time-by-anthony-powell/">http://anzlitlovers.com/2012/01/22/a-...</a></span>

<div id="review_50116244" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="Ed" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/222151-ed"><img alt="Ed" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1196778782p2/222151.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/50116244?book_show_action=true">Mar 22, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ed" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ed" href="/user/show/222151-ed">Ed</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer50116244" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16285364396905410154">This and the other four volumes are actually a total of 12 novels following a welter of British characters from 1914 until the mid 1960s. I am about to start reading the whole sequence for the third time. There is also a great BBC dramatization on DVD: Dance to the Music of Time.<br/><br/>This is the British equivalent of Proust's In Search of Lost Time. I guess I find it closer to life as it was lived in the 20th century and certainly to the idea of our lives as a dance that characters keep returning to,</span>
  <span id="freeText16285364396905410154" style="display:none">This and the other four volumes are actually a total of 12 novels following a welter of British characters from 1914 until the mid 1960s. I am about to start reading the whole sequence for the third time. There is also a great BBC dramatization on DVD: Dance to the Music of Time.<br /><br />This is the British equivalent of Proust's In Search of Lost Time. I guess I find it closer to life as it was lived in the 20th century and certainly to the idea of our lives as a dance that characters keep returning to, in different changing guises, mirroring society's evolution as well as their personal characters.<br /><br />In Widmerpool Powell has created an awesomely representative villain whose growing evil is perfectly understandable given his starting point and what happens around him. Though memorably someone once said: 'we are all someone's Widmerpool': someone thinks us evil in some way. And he has one of my favorite tragic characters: Charles Stringham. But it is the way minor characters pop up over the 50 year trajectory that I find fun. And also finding out the real people Powell based his characters on: which includes George Orwell.<br /><br />Anyway if you have six months reading to find: this novel sequence is something to try. </span>
  <a data-text-id="16285364396905410154" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_943702373" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/943702373" />
    <a title="Nigeyb" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10301421-nigeyb"><img alt="Nigeyb" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1593065725p2/10301421.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/943702373?book_show_action=true">May 21, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nigeyb" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nigeyb" href="/user/show/10301421-nigeyb">Nigeyb</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780099436683" href="/book/show/1179970.A_Dance_to_the_Music_of_Time_Volume_1">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer943702373" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5890545437715156833"><i>"A Dance to the Music of Time"</i> is a twelve-volume cycle of novels by Anthony Powell. The books are available individually or as four volumes.<br/><br/><b>Spring</b><br/>A Question of Upbringing (1951)<br/>A Buyer's Market (1952)<br/>The Acceptance World (1955)<br/><br/><b>Summer</b><br/>At Lady Molly's (1957)<br/>Casanova's Chinese Restaurant (1960)<br/>The Kindly Ones (1962)<br/><br/><b>Autumn</b><br/>The Valley of Bones (1964)<br/>The Soldier's Art (1966)<br/>The Military Philosophers (1968)<br/><br/><b>Winter</b><br/>Books Do Furnish a Room (1971)<br/>Temporary Kings (1973)<br/>Hearing Secret Harmonies (1975)<br/><br/>(Dates ar</span>
  <span id="freeText5890545437715156833" style="display:none"><i>"A Dance to the Music of Time"</i> is a twelve-volume cycle of novels by Anthony Powell. The books are available individually or as four volumes.<br><br><b>Spring</b><br>A Question of Upbringing (1951)<br>A Buyer's Market (1952)<br>The Acceptance World (1955)<br><br><b>Summer</b><br>At Lady Molly's (1957)<br>Casanova's Chinese Restaurant (1960)<br>The Kindly Ones (1962)<br><br><b>Autumn</b><br>The Valley of Bones (1964)<br>The Soldier's Art (1966)<br>The Military Philosophers (1968)<br><br><b>Winter</b><br>Books Do Furnish a Room (1971)<br>Temporary Kings (1973)<br>Hearing Secret Harmonies (1975)<br><br>(Dates are first UK publication dates)<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1400100818i/9634923.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><i>This twelve-volume sequence <b>A Dance to the Music of Time</b> traces a colourful group of English acquaintances from 1914 to 1971. The slowly developing narrative centres around life's poignant encounters between friends and lovers who later drift apart and yet keep reencountering each other over numerous unfolding decades as they move through the vicissitudes of marriage, work, aging, and ultimately death. <br><br>Until the last three volumes, the standard excitements of old-fashioned plots (What will happen next? Will x marry y? Will y murder z?) seem far less important than time's slow reshuffling of friends, acquaintances, and lovers in intricate human arabesques."</i><br><br><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/399549.Time_and_Anthony_Powell_A_Critical_Study" title="Time and Anthony Powell A Critical Study by Robert L. Selig" rel="nofollow noopener">Time and Anthony Powell: A Critical Study</a> by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/226666.Robert_L__Selig" title="Robert L. Selig" rel="nofollow noopener">Robert L. Selig</a><br><br><br>Below is a review of each of the three novels that are contained within "<b>Spring</b>".<br><br>* * * * *<br><br><u>1. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/16116.A_Question_of_Upbringing__A_Dance_to_the_Music_of_Time___1_" title="A Question of Upbringing (A Dance to the Music of Time, #1) by Anthony Powell" rel="nofollow noopener">A Question of Upbringing</a> (Volume 1)</u>: I've wanted to read "A Dance to the Music of Time" since discovering that Julian Maclaren-Ross features somewhere in the series as a character called X. Trapnel. Such is my interest inJulian Maclaren-Ross (I am, of course, assuming you have already read "Fear and Loathing in Fitzrovia: The Bizarre Life of Writer, Actor, Soho Raconteur Julian Maclaren-Ross" by Paul Willetts) that this is sufficient to inspire me to tackle one of the longest works of fiction in English literature. You probably feel exactly the same. If you don't then you should consider it.<br><br>Published in 1951, "A Question of Upbringing" is the reminiscences of Nick Jenkins (presumably based on Powell himself) who recounts his last few years at public school around 1921, a summer spent in France, and then onto university. It's a familiar world of gilded privilege, akin to the early sections of "Brideshead Revisited", though with very little by way of drama or narrative. Instead the reader is introduced to a variety of disparate characters and some prescient anecdotes. I say prescient as Jenkins hints at the ways in which their lives will turn out.<br><br>What makes this book a delight is the beautiful writing, which really captures the era and milieu, and which is aligned to regular doses of humour. Powell captures the transition of adolescence into adulthood perfectly: the insecurities, the naivety, the fast changes, the gaucheness, the way friendships may evolve and fracture, and how life choices made at this stage can shape whole lives.<br><br>I suspect this series will get better and better and "A Question of Upbringing" lays the groundwork for what it is to follow. I cannot wait to find out. 4/5<br><br><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/357902930" rel="nofollow noopener">Click here to see my review of A Question of Upbringing (A Dance to the Music of Time #1)</a><br><br>* * * * *<br><br><u>2. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1006997.A_Buyer_s_Market__A_Dance_to_the_Music_of_Time__2_" title="A Buyer's Market (A Dance to the Music of Time #2) by Anthony Powell" rel="nofollow noopener">A Buyer's Market</a> (Volume 2)</u>: "A Buyer's Market" is the second book in Anthony Powell's twelve novel sequence "A Dance To The Music of Time" and it picks up the narrative in 1928, via a flashback to Paris where narrator Nick Jenkins introduces us to an artist called Mr Deacon.<br><br>Nick is now in his early twenties and whilst more grown up, still uncertain of his place in the world. I assume this explains the book's title. Nick and his contemporaries are searching for money, jobs, sex, social status etc. and their search takes them to a succession of social events that Nick recounts in the same first hand manner of "A Question of Upbringing". Also, in common with "A Question of Upbringing", it's full of day-to-day detail and Nick's perception of those he encounters.<br><br>How reliable is Nick as a narrator? He frequently revises his opinions about those he meets not least Widmerpool whose personal journey continues apace. The narrative technique adds to the sense of surprise and gives the book a few memorable twists. For me Widmerpool is very much the star of the show and I love the way he kept turning up in ever more incongruous and unexpected places - constantly surprising and confounding Nick Jenkins.<br><br>There are also, and again in common with the first book, some moments of sublime humour, and much of the exquisite writing has a pleasing and playful tone.<br><br>I enjoyed this book every bit as much as "A Question of Upbringing", and now look forward to reading the third instalment, "The Acceptance World".<br><br>Reading the first two instalments of "A Dance To The Music of Time" has been an absolute joy and akin to the pleasure of slipping into a hot bath. I recommend both books and eagerly anticipate completing the journey from 1914 to 1971 - and discovering what happens to Nick and his group. 4/5<br><br><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/931676022" rel="nofollow noopener">Click here to see my review of A Buyer's Market (A Dance to the Music of Time #2)</a><br><br>* * * * *<br><br><u>3. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1376765.The_Acceptance_World__A_Dance_to_the_Music_of_Time__3_" title="The Acceptance World (A Dance to the Music of Time #3) by Anthony Powell" rel="nofollow noopener">The Acceptance World</a> (Volume 3)</u>: Ten years on from the start of A Question of Upbringing, which starts with narrator Nick Jenkins recounting his last few years at public school, The Acceptance World begins in London in 1931. The recurring characters have all undergone significant change. Some that seemed to have the world at their feet have squandered their gifts, whilst others who were more pitiful in their younger incarnations are enjoying success.<br><br>The economic gloom that characterised the 1930s seems to permeate this part of "A Dance to the Music of Time", despite this, The Acceptance World is supremely enjoyable.<br><br>This volume introduces a hint of mysticism, a new character called Myra Erdleigh tells Nicks fortune through the ancient practice of cartomancy (reading ordinary playing cards) and, later in the story, there is an incident with a planchette. I'd never heard of them before but apparently planchettes came to prominence in the years following the establishment of Spiritualism in America in the mid-nineteenth century, kick starting a craze for supernaturally tinged parlour games, séances etc. that must have continued into the early 1930s (probably with a resurgence after World War One).<br><br>The Acceptance World is certainly the most dramatic volume so far, with a merry-go-round of relationship change. Almost as soon as two disparate characters meet there's a likelihood of a change in relationship status in the offing.<br><br>As I work through the series it is becoming clearer how some of what happens in the early stages, sets up the Dance as we move forward in time. This is making the series progressively more enjoyable, rewarding and compelling. I raced through The Acceptance World such was the pleasure it gave me. I've enjoyed all of the first three volumes however this has been the most enjoyable so far.<br><br>I encourage anyone who starts the series, and is unsure about whether to commit, to stick with it. It becomes ever more enjoyable and rewarding.<br><br>My other tip is to refer to AnthonyPowell.org where there are lists that help the reader to keep track of who is who, along with a synopsis of every volume, and interesting and insightful essays. 5/5<br><br><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/940630320" rel="nofollow noopener">Click here to see my review of The Acceptance World (A Dance to the Music of Time #3)</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="5890545437715156833" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_18307209" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/18307209" />
    <a title="Gary Lee" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/996286-gary-lee"><img alt="Gary Lee" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1327814927p2/996286.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/18307209?book_show_action=true">Mar 21, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Gary Lee" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Gary Lee" href="/user/show/996286-gary-lee">Gary Lee</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">armchair anglophiles</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/996286-gary-lee?shelf=overlooked-classics">overlooked-classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/996286-gary-lee?shelf=brit-lit">brit-lit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/996286-gary-lee?shelf=literatti">literatti</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer18307209" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16869104135253392122">This contains the first three novels of Powell's cycle.<br/><br/><i>A Question of Upbringing</i> -- 4 out of 5 stars.<br/>This first novel, of the overall twelve novels involved, comes across as little more than a high(er)-brow version of <i>A Seperate Peace</i>. And to me, that's not a bad thing. It's quite readable, if a bit dry in places, and manages itself very well.<br/><br/>It's essentially the first (230page) chapter of an overall novel that spans the life of the main character; so, this time is spent introducing the character</span>
  <span id="freeText16869104135253392122" style="display:none">This contains the first three novels of Powell's cycle.<br /><br /><i>A Question of Upbringing</i> -- 4 out of 5 stars.<br />This first novel, of the overall twelve novels involved, comes across as little more than a high(er)-brow version of <i>A Seperate Peace</i>. And to me, that's not a bad thing. It's quite readable, if a bit dry in places, and manages itself very well.<br /><br />It's essentially the first (230page) chapter of an overall novel that spans the life of the main character; so, this time is spent introducing the character, as well as detailing certain important bits of his adolescence and young adult life. Not much more. There are eleven more novels to fill in the rest.<br />I think that's where most readers get annoyed. If one comes to each novel in this cycle, expecting a definite beginning/middle/end structure, they won't really find it. While each book is able to stand on its own, it's the entire cycle that is meant to be read as one single (very long) novel.<br /><br /><br /><i>A Buyer's Market</i> -- 4/5 stars<br />A bit more involved and engaging than the first novel.<br />This second novel finds the main character (and subsequent supporting characters) a few years older, and not much wiser. They've now finished with University and are starting to make their first appearances in the London social scene. That's about it for this one -- as with the previous novel, this one follows episode after episode of Jenkins' young adult life, but only going from one social episode to the next -- one party or weekend outing after another.<br />One character is introduced in the first chapter, dead in the last chapter.<br />There's a questionable abortion -- questionable in that it might have been a scheme for money.<br />Homoerotic overtone, heteroerotic overtones -- it's all here. Just as in life.<br /><br /><i>The Acceptance World</i> -- 5/5 stars<br />The first two novels set everything up -- the major characters, themes, motifs, history, mythology, etc. And now, Powell starts to get into the beginning of the heart of the narrative. The characters are now taking jobs, marrying, having affairs, divorcing, becoming drunks, becoming major players in society, becoming social revolutionaries -- in part, they're growing older and finally interacting with the world around them.<br />I'm quick to write this one off as the best novel of the three collected in this first volume, but I'm not sure if it's because it actually is the best of the three or because I've become familiar with all of the main characters and have become interested in their lives and interactions.<br /><br /><br /><br /><br />Starting 7/1/08, I'm giving myself one year to make it through Powell's cycle.<br />We'll see how it goes...</span>
  <a data-text-id="16869104135253392122" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_97872950" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/97872950" />
    <a title="carl  theaker" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3381893-carl-theaker"><img alt="carl  theaker" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1368717702p2/3381893.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/97872950?book_show_action=true">Apr 10, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="carl  theaker" class="user" itemprop="url" name="carl  theaker" href="/user/show/3381893-carl-theaker">carl  theaker</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to carl  by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/3377227-r">R.</a>
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3381893-carl-theaker?shelf=modern-library-100">modern-library-100</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer97872950" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17044142838877346221">12 novellas, oh, about 3000 pages, covering 50 years of London life with the who's who, socialites, writers, b-list celebrities, politicos, and historical figures all thrown in, most of whom would<br/>be unknown to any contemporary Londoner let alone the rest of us, written by a snooty erudite, somehow it all works.<br/><br/>If you're in it for the long haul, I suggest an 'Invitation to the Dance' by Hillary Spurling, a guide and glossary to all that is about to appear before you.<br/><br/>The first 3 books contained in</span>
  <span id="freeText17044142838877346221" style="display:none">12 novellas, oh, about 3000 pages, covering 50 years of London life with the who's who, socialites, writers, b-list celebrities, politicos, and historical figures all thrown in, most of whom would<br />be unknown to any contemporary Londoner let alone the rest of us, written by a snooty erudite, somehow it all works.<br /><br />If you're in it for the long haul, I suggest an 'Invitation to the Dance' by Hillary Spurling, a guide and glossary to all that is about to appear before you.<br /><br />The first 3 books contained in Spring introduce Nick Jenkins/Powell, the narrator, and his compatriots at college in the 1920s. Jenkins becomes a reviewer, creator and appreciator of the visual arts, like Powell in real life, and uses famous paintings symbolically throughout the series, thus the title 'A Dance to the Music of Time'.<br /><br />Like all good English novels everybody endures school and everyone has family money of some level to sustain them. Now that I think about it, only in DH Lawrence works do the characters ever get their hands dirty.<br /><br />Powell begins a steady supply of odd characters who give Jenkins something to compare, contrast and be bemused by. These characters come and go, giving a cohesion over the dance of time.<br /><br />The boys are always thinking about the girls and Jenkins falls in love fairly regularly in that genteel way that no one, outside the reader, ever knows.<br /><br />Life gets rolling by the third book 'Acceptance World' where just about everyone gets married and almost all divorced.<br /><br />It's all good reading in a MasterPiece theater way; the Great Depression is a having some effect on the characters, but is handled as a subtle backdrop to personal relationships and political rantings in Hyde Park.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17044142838877346221" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_101758672" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/101758672" />
    <a title="Timothy Hallinan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1619829-timothy-hallinan"><img alt="Timothy Hallinan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442462926p2/1619829.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/101758672?book_show_action=true">May 07, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Timothy Hallinan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Timothy Hallinan" href="/user/show/1619829-timothy-hallinan">Timothy Hallinan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer101758672" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10473318384139852652">My favorite novel of the 20th century is probably Anthony Powell's twelve-volume marathon, A DANCE TO THE MUSIC OF TIME, written between 1951 and 1975. Supremely civilized, enormous in design, an unforgettable picture of a way of life (and a class) that were disappearing even when Powell was one of the "bright young people" who were so visible in the 1920s in London, the books that make up Dance are also very funny.<br/><br/>I first read DANCE when I was in my early thirties, and the story (in the first t</span>
  <span id="freeText10473318384139852652" style="display:none">My favorite novel of the 20th century is probably Anthony Powell's twelve-volume marathon, A DANCE TO THE MUSIC OF TIME, written between 1951 and 1975. Supremely civilized, enormous in design, an unforgettable picture of a way of life (and a class) that were disappearing even when Powell was one of the "bright young people" who were so visible in the 1920s in London, the books that make up Dance are also very funny.<br /><br />I first read DANCE when I was in my early thirties, and the story (in the first three books) of the friendship of three boys thrown together in school and the gradual dissolution of those friendships as the world calls the young men in different directions, meant a great deal to me at the time. I'd never read anything that seemed to speak so directly to my own life. This was emphasized by the loss, in the book, of one boy -- the most brilliant one -- into a life of drink and another into sexual dissipation that ruins his relationships and irremediably coarsens his character. I had watched several friends hit the rocks by that time and had sailed pretty close to them myself.<br /><br />The remaining books chronicle the irresistible rise of the boy the others had scorned, the implacable Widmerpool, who amasses power almost as revenge for being unloved and unliked, and who demonstrates a resilience to humiliation -- even sexual humiliation -- that's almost mythical in scope. I think Widmerpool may be the fictional creation I most admire. <br /><br />By the way, Powell's four-volume memoir, TO KEEP THE BALL ROLLING, also speaks to a level of civilization that seems today to be as lost to us as Atlantis.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10473318384139852652" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_50130346" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/50130346" />
    <a title="James" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/426277-james"><img alt="James" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1584954318p2/426277.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/50130346?book_show_action=true">Mar 22, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="James" class="user" itemprop="url" name="James" href="/user/show/426277-james">James</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/426277-james?shelf=lp2000s">lp2000s</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer50130346" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2450888716037413617">Powell takes you back to a time and place, Britain and France in the 1920s, that no longer exists. He also describes a class culture that is unfamiliar to this reader who grew up in the Midwest. He does this with a prose style and a structure that, through episodes in the lives of four boys on the verge of adulthood, slowly builds a story that seems very true to life. You gradually learn about the relationships through the eys of the narrator, Jenkins, and by the time he says goodbye to his Uncl</span>
  <span id="freeText2450888716037413617" style="display:none">Powell takes you back to a time and place, Britain and France in the 1920s, that no longer exists. He also describes a class culture that is unfamiliar to this reader who grew up in the Midwest. He does this with a prose style and a structure that, through episodes in the lives of four boys on the verge of adulthood, slowly builds a story that seems very true to life. You gradually learn about the relationships through the eys of the narrator, Jenkins, and by the time he says goodbye to his Uncle Giles at the end of the first volume, A Question of Upbringing, you have become engaged with these individuals, their loves and dreams for the future.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2450888716037413617" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1413058856" />
    <a title="Mark Joyce" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/42499758-mark-joyce"><img alt="Mark Joyce" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1479657688p2/42499758.jpg" /></a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1413058856" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2895455771834219293">A book of this length will inevitably divide opinion but I’m firmly with those who consider it a subtle, under-stated masterpiece worthy of comparison with The Great Gatsby and Brideshead Revisited. It goes into much greater detail than either of those two books on the social dynamics of debutant balls, country house parties and London private members’ clubs and as such may try the patience of those with a limited appetite for that kind of thing. But, like Fitzgerald and Waugh, Powell is really </span>
  <span id="freeText2895455771834219293" style="display:none">A book of this length will inevitably divide opinion but I’m firmly with those who consider it a subtle, under-stated masterpiece worthy of comparison with The Great Gatsby and Brideshead Revisited. It goes into much greater detail than either of those two books on the social dynamics of debutant balls, country house parties and London private members’ clubs and as such may try the patience of those with a limited appetite for that kind of thing. But, like Fitzgerald and Waugh, Powell is really writing about the loss of innocence and the fragility of human relationships. For my money he achieves an impact just as powerful as those two greats. I don’t mind admitting there were a couple of points at which I had to blink and cough hard into a clenched fist in order to maintain a manly disposition. </span>
  <a data-text-id="2895455771834219293" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <span id="freeText480655526167840389" style="display:none">I count the Dance to the Music of Time as one of the most important literary achievements of the 20th century. I've read the entire sequence of novels twice, and found the second reading a richer experiences than the first, such is the density and complexity of Powell's amazing achievement. Powell has created the richest and most detailed fictional narrative in the English language, in my opinion. I will read the series a third time in a few years, because there is much hidden in the story, I feel, that can only be reached after a basic familiarity with the text has been achieved.</span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('xj9kmwzwUIbJRFnb97gK11TBciiHr0EbgQRPdtrbHcdOdjKKQUST8lVw2H+1nMYDJ44LpYwE2c44ToIC4Z1Ayg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {

  }

<script>
//<![CDATA[
      var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_36978223'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/36978223-anthony-powell?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">Anthony Powell: Dancing to the Music of Time<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/118313.Hilary_Spurling?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">Hilary Spurling<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p3\"><\/span><\/span> 4.18 avg rating &mdash; 111 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 2017\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer18445352997100449915\">The author of the award-winning, two-volume Matisse: A Life, now gives us the long-awaited, definitive biography of literary master Anthony Powell--the critic, editor, and novelist known as \"the English Proust\"-- that, at the same time, takes us deep<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText18445352997100449915\" style=\"display:none\">The author of the award-winning, two-volume Matisse: A Life, now gives us the long-awaited, definitive biography of literary master Anthony Powell--the critic, editor, and novelist known as &quot;the English Proust&quot;-- that, at the same time, takes us deep into twentieth-century London literary life.\n\nAnthony Powell (1905-2000), best known for his twelve-volume comic masterpiece, A Dance to the Music of Time,  was also the author of sixteen earlier novels, plays, and biographies, five memoirs, and three volumes of journals. He was a prolific literary critic and book reviewer. Between the two world wars, before making his name, he kept company with rowdy, hard-up writers and painters--and painters\' models--in the London where Augustus John and Wyndham Lewis loomed large. He counted Evelyn Waugh and Henry Green among his lifelong friends, and his circle included the Sitwells, Graham Greene, George Orwell, Philip Larkin, and Kingsley Amis, among many others. Now, drawing on his letters, diaries, and interviews, Hilary Spurling--herself a longtime friend of Powell\'s-- has written a fresh and masterful portrait of the man, his work, and his time. Insightful, poignant, and cinematic in scope, this biography is as much a brilliant tapestry of a seminal moment in London\'s literary life as it is a revelation of an iconic literary <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer11104760912010679373">Anthony Dymoke Powell CH, CBE was an English novelist best known for his twelve-volume work A Dance to the Music of Time, published between 1951 and 1975.<br/>Powell's major work has remained in print continuously and has been the subject of TV and radio dramatisations. In 2008, The Times newspaper named Powell among their list of "The 50 greatest British writers since 1945".</span>
